From grannddaughter Tina Newton:


"Newport, NH: George Elbridge Watling, 93, passed away at the Woodlawn Nursing Home, Wednesday, December 13, 2006

George was born to J. Geoerge and Emma (Ronan) Watling on November 24, 1913 in Augusta, GA. In 1919 he moved with his family to this area and grew up in Grantham and Newport attending school and graduating from Newport. He was employed at the Gordon Woolen Mill for 10 years and at the Dorr Woolen Mill for 25 years prior to his retirement in 1984. He was a WWII Naval Veteran and a long standing member of the Odd Fellows, the American Legion and the South Congregational Church.

Survivors include two daughters: Christine Newton of Colorado Springs, CO, and Sarah Jane Gunzinger of Perkinsville, VT, 2 grandchildren and several nieces and nephews. George was predeceased by his firs wife Laila (Anderson) who passed away in 1988 and his second wife Helen (Neimi) Hokkanen whom he married in 1994. Helen passed away in October of 2004. A graveside service will take place in the spring. Thhere will be no calling hours or service at this time. Memorial donations may be made to the Newport Senior Cemter."

98. GRACE ELIZABETH6 GLENDENNING (JANE ELIZABETH JARDINE5 WATLING, JUBAL4, JUBAL3, JUBAL2, WATLING1 FAMILY)1 was born September 23, 1911 in Parksville, Tennessee, USA1, and died August 9, 1991 in Winthrop, Mass. U. S. A.1. She married LEROY MORRISON SMITH1 August 26, 1936 in St. Pauls Presbyterian Church, Black River, NB1, son of ALBERT SMITH and ELLEN MORRISON. He was born June 22, 1908 in Malden, USA1, and died October 2, 1985 in Malden, Mass., USA1.


Notes for GRACE ELIZABETH GLENDENNING:

Lillian said that Grace attended Armstrong School in Parksville, Tenn


Grace was the oldest in the family and was born in Parksville, Tennessee. Dad and Mum had set up housekeeping there in February 1911 where Dad worked on Ocoee #1, the first of three dams build on the Ocoee River. Aunt Janet had journeyed to Parksville to be with Mum when Grace was born.


When Grace was young, Dad and Mum moved from construction site to construction site in the US and, presumably, Grace attended school wherever she was; one school was the Armstrong School but I am not sure if it was in Connecticut or Virginia. The family returned to NB in 1919 and, again, presumably Grace attended the Little Branch School. Later she attended Success Business College in Moncton, NB where she took a business program. While she made good marks, apparently she did not like the program. It was when returning from Business College with Grace, that Dad upset the Model T at Gilbert's Corner, near Shediac. Grace broke her wrist but even so, went to a dance in the Little Branch School that night.


Like so many other Maritimers, Grace went to Boston to work, probably in 1930 and worked for various families caring for children and likely cooking. . On her first trip, she traveled with Mrs. F. A. Fowlie and Ernest MacLean who were going to Lowell, Mass to visit relatives. She may have she stayed for a while with Dad's cousin, Sam Knowles. At one time she lived at 1595 Beacon Street, Beverley, Mass. She frequently visited her Uncle Jack Watling in Boston and later when he lived on Swan's Island near Eastport, Maine. Grace returned regularly to Little Branch for the summer coming sometimes by steamer from Boston to Saint John, NB. (I believe that the fare to Saint John was $10.00) In 1947, when I left home for Teachers' College in Fredericton, I inherited Grace's steamer trunk - and it carried my belongings on a number of later trips.


As a young woman in Little Branch and later in Boston, Grace loved to square dance; Lillian said that the night she landed in Boston in 1933, she went to a dance with Grace. Perhaps that is where she met Roy Morrison Smith (1908-1985). Roy, I believe, preferred to round dance. She and Roy were married in St. Paul's Presbyterian Church on August 26, 1936. In 1936, Walter, Dad, Elinor and I drove to Boston to bring Grace home. After marriage, Grace and Roy lived with Roy's mother in Saugus, Mass, later moving to 23 Spring Street in Malden. While in Saugus, Grace worked for Chisholm's Motel on Route 1. One evening while standing on the sidewalk near her home in Malden talking to a neighbour, a man came along, knocked her off her feet and stole her purse.



Grace, Roy and family were frequent visitors to Little Branch and even when they could not come, their children made the trip. My recollection is that Barbara and Lawrence spent 16 consecutive summers with their Grandmother at Little Branch. Often they came by train but also frequently traveled with expatriate New Brunswick folk who were visiting family in the Miramichi. I can remember being wakened in the middle of the night when Grace arrived home. She had brought some clothes for me and wanted to see them on me. The story is told that one summer when Grace was home and helping with the cooking and Ken was a toddler, Grace would jam Ken's dress in the pantry door in order to keep him out of her way. In later years, Grace frequently landed home carrying shopping bags with fresh tomatoes from Roy's garden, bagels for Jean MacKenzie and the usual things for family members.


Grace had extraordinary eyesight. While walking through a field she could reach down and pick a four leaf clover. One time she entered a contest, which required her to count the dots in a picture of a TV. She won, and then decided she should count the dots again in case she was asked the number at the presentation ceremony. I know that she also won other contests but cannot recall which ones. As a youngster, I can recall going with Grace and others to pick blueberries and raspberries and of walking through the fields. I am not sure if we were going somewhere or just for a walk.

Although she left New Brunswick as a young woman, she did not lose interest in family, friends or the community. She subscribed to the Chatham Gazette for many years and was well informed about activities among her extended family. She was proud of her family, fiercely loyal, generous to a fault and she and Roy always welcomed and provided shelter for the many visiting family members. She was quick to arrange for visitors to see the Museum of Science, visit down town Boston, see the Saugus Iron works, etc.


During her later years Grace had a form of multiple sclerosis and had difficulty walking. She then moved to Cliffside Nursing Home and Winthrop, Mass. She is buried in the Black River Cemetery.

She was predeceased by her husband, Roy, in 1985 and by a brother, Joh

Notes for LEROY MORRISON SMITH:

Roy was born in Malden, Mass. where he attended public school and graduated from Malden High School. Roy worked for nearly 20 years for Benjamin Moore Paints, and then moved to the Englander Mattress Co. where he worked as a mattress retiring in 1973. Following retirement, he worked for a number of years in retail sales at Sears in Malden

Roy was very active in St. Paul's Episcopal Church, and was a member of the retired Men's Club of the Church. During his younger years, he was an avid horseshoe player participating and winning in competitions throughout the state. His hobbies included model trains and coin collection. Roy also enjoyed travel to New Brunswick where he enjoyed the peace and quiet of the countryside and visiting family and friends.

99. WALTER JUBAL6 GLENDENNING (JANE ELIZABETH JARDINE5 WATLING, JUBAL4, JUBAL3, JUBAL2, WATLING1 FAMILY)1 was born September 29, 1913 in Black River Bridge, NB1, and died June 16, 1998 in Moncton Hospital, Moncton, NB. He married ELSPETH AGNES GIBSON1 November 1, 1939 in Presbyterian Manse, Boom Road, New Brunswick1, daughter of JOHN GIBSON and ELSPETH FINNO. She was born May 22, 1912 in Black River Bridge , NB1, and died June 8, 2003.


Notes for WALTER JUBAL GLENDENNING:

Walter was born in New Brunswick; Mother had come back from Tennessee to New Brunswick for his birth and may have stayed in NB until late 1916 - we do not know for sure. The family returned permanently to Little Branch in 1919 and it is likely that is where Walter started school -. It is likely that he only went to school for 6-8 years and then would have worked with Dad on the farm.


Walter did not talk much about his early years but I know that he worked long hours both summer and winter. Because Dad was such a worker and Walter was the oldest boy in the family, I am sure that a great deal was expected of him. He had a team of dogs, Chowder and Pup, which he hitched to a sled in winter and traveled around the community, and cross to visit Alex Newton. Once he took Dad across to George Adams' to use the phone. The longest trip, Walter said, was to John W. MacNaughton's - a distance of about 7 miles.


Canobie and Black River Glendenning family members kept in touch and visited back and forth. One time, the Christmas, 1938 I believe, Walter was taking family members to Chatham in the Model A. The right front window was broken and was replaced with a piece of cardboard. Seems to me that Mum, Ken, Norm, Lillian and I were in the car. As we neared the railroad tracks, Walter spotted a train approaching from the right. He tried to stop but the car kept sliding towards the tracks. Quick thinking, he tramped on the gas instead and got across just ahead of the train. People on the back of Adams' truck just ahead of us were sure that we were going to be hit.


Walter began building a home on the Dan MacLean place in 1937 and moved into it on November 1, 1940. In the meantime, they lived at Little Branch. The basement was dug with the aid of a horse, Jess, and a scoop and most if not all of the lumber came from Hallidays. The original barn on the property burned in 1932, the same year as the Black River Hall. His house was struck by lightning in 1940 but did not burn.


Although Walter farmed, he also worked off the farm. In 1940 he worked at clearing the bombing range along the Loggieville Road. He was called to the Army Service Corps during the war and was stationed at Ottawa, Halifax, Debert and Camp Borden. On his return, he continued to farm and to work at the airport. In 1950 he commenced work with Modern Construction Co. Ltd. In 1959 he moved to Moncton and continued to work for Modern at job sites throughout New Brunswick including their head office in Moncton. He retired in 1978.


Dad was one of the promoters of farmers working together and was Secretary Treasurer of the Agricultural Society #103. Part of the role was to maintain a "store" from which seeds, etc. would be sold. After Dad died, Walter took over the role and moved the shed from the Branch to his place.


Walter and Agnes always welcomed visitors - family and friends alike - and they were good hosts. A meal seemed to appear effortlessly and Walter encouraged visitors to "eat up and give the house a good name." He liked to travel and visit family and friends in Bathurst, Rexton, Harvey Station and down to the Boston area. He also traveled widely stopping to see men he had worked with. For a while they owned a cottage Florina Beach near Cocagne, NB.


Walter is also remembered for his keen memory. He seemed to remember anyone he ever worked with, perhaps even met, and would visit if he was passing nearby. He enjoyed reminiscing but talked little about what himself.


Walter loved to visit the old home, "The Branch." A few years ago, when he was not able to drive, I arranged to pick him up for a day's outing. When we got into the car I said, "We can go any where you would like. Would you like to go to the Island? Saint John? Fredericton? Springhill? Or where?" He hesitated only for a moment then said, "I think that I'd like to go to the Branch". .

Notes for ELSPETH AGNES GIBSON:

Agnes was born in Black River and attended the Middle Black River (Cameron) school and was a member of St. Paul's Presbyterian Church; like so many young women, Agnes went to Boston to work (She had relatives there) but came home most summers. I remember one year of Agnes coming to our place driving her brother's (?) car. Cannot remember the make but it would be a 1930-something with extra wheels mounted on each front fender complete with silver spokes and whitewall tires.


In the late 1950's, Walters work transferred him to Moncton. Shortly thereafter, Agnes enrolled in the RNA program at the New Brunswick Institute of Technology; she worked at the Moncton Hospital retiring in 1976. Agnes also had an eye for antiques.

100. VENETIA KATHERINE6 GLENDENNING (JANE ELIZABETH JARDINE5 WATLING, JUBAL4, JUBAL3, JUBAL2, WATLING1 FAMILY)1 was born May 14, 1915 in Little Branch, New Brunswick1, and died January 11, 2003. She married DONALD CAMERON RUSSELL1 August 4, 1937 in St. Pauls, Black River, New Brunswick1, son of ERNEST RUSSELL and CHRISTINA CAMERON. He was born December 29, 1906 in Point Aux Carr, NB ?1, and died August 22, 1979 in Point Aux Carr, NB1.


Notes for VENETIA KATHERINE GLENDENNING:

Venetia was born at Little Branch. Mum and Dad were living in Little Branch at the time. Venetia attended the Little Branch School for Grades 1-8, the highest grade available in the Little Branch School, and then she

stayed with Aunt Tine in Loggieville where she completed Grade nine. Following that, she wrote Entrance Exams and gained First Class Entrance to the Provincial Normal School from which she graduated in 1933. As I recall, Venetia taught in New Carlysle, Napan, Penniac and Bayside; she may also have taught somewhere north of Fredericton. She also taught in Point Aux Carr, Little Branch, Miramichi Rural High School and Chatham retiring in 1975. Quite a number of people at her wake mentioned that Venetia had been their teacher.


Venetia was active in her church, St. Paul's Presbyterian. She probably attended Sunday school at St. Stephen's Presbyterian Church prior to it becoming St. Stephen's United Church. When St. Paul's opened in 1930, there was a PYPS rally prior to the church opening. And Venetia was President of the Young Peoples Society. The local paper, presumably the Chatham Gazette, noted, "Miss Venetia Glendenning, President Black River Y.P.S, in a very pleasing manner welcomed the delegates…" Venetia was a member of St. Paul's since its establishment, played the organ for many years and for 40 years was Church Treasurer. She was also a member of the Women's Missionary Society and the Women's League.


Venetia met Cameron Russell (1906-1979) in the spring of 1932 at Little Branch. Cam was rafting logs for Harrison Murdoch and they tied up at the wharf. Cam and perhaps other members of the crew came over to talk to the girls in the schoolyard. They married in St. Paul's Presbyterian Church and lived in Point Aux Carr in a home they bought from Mrs. Simon MacDonald. Mrs. MacDonald retained a room which she used mainly in the summer. Cam and Venetia had three children, Glenn, Leonard and Donna.


Venetia was an avid reader, a good listener and a good sport. She was a good conversationalist but not one to gossip or be critical of others. She demanded nothing, asked little and offered much. She did not complain and was always "fine" when asked about her own health. She did not want to bother people. She could entertain herself and did not mind living alone. In fact, she lived alone for 20 years after she became a widow.


Venetia was a resource for information about her community and we shared an interest in genealogy; she provided me with much of my initial information about the Watling side of the family. She could recall family relationships from memory whereas I have to rely on my notes and records.


Venetia and Cam had many trips to Florida with Ken and Verne. When lighting her stove to warm the house on return from one trip in 1989 or 1990, the house caught fire and burned. She rebuilt on the same site. Venetia and Cam visited Donna and Vernon when they lived in Vancouver and later in England and, while in England, traveled with the family by car around Europe.


As a youngster, I recall the nice table Venetia set at mealtime. A visit to Venetia's always ended in lunch before leaving. Sometimes when visiting the Miramichi in recent years, I would drop in on Venetia's about 8:00 am and enjoy toast, jam, tea and conversation. She kept scotch cookies in her freezer and always set some out when she knew I was coming for a visit.



Venetia boarded with Archie MacKay in Fredericton while attending Normal School.

The web site of Adams Funeral Home of January 16, 2003 included the following:

"Mrs Venetia K. Russell

Miramichi - Mrs. Venetia Kathrine Russell, widow of the late Cameron Russell, of the Miramichi Senior Citizens Home, and formerly of Point Aux Carr, passed away at the Miramichi Regional Hospital, on Saturday morning, January 11, 2003 at the age of 87.

Born in Black River Bridge, she was the daughter of the late Elmer A, and Jane (Watling) Glendenning. She was a teacher by profession; a member of the N.B. Retired Teaches Assoc., a life member of the Miarmichi Teachers Assoc.; a member of St. Pauls Presbyterian Church, Black River Bridge; a former treasurer of that church, for 40 years as well as a former organist; a member of the Missionary Society; as well as a well known community historian.

She is survived by one daughter, Mrs Donna Goodfellow (Vernon). of China; two sons, Glenn Russell (Marilyn), of Miramichi, and Leonard Russell (Glenna) of Graham's Road, PEI; three brothers, Kenneth Glendenning (Vern), of Napan, Norman Glendenning (Jean), of Black River Bridge, and Donald Glendenning (Carol) of Charlottetown, P.E.I.; six grandchildren,Janet, Anne, Amy Lynn and Cameron Goodfellow, Susanne and Joh Russell; one great grandchild, Theora Holden; as well as numerous nieces and nephews. She was predeceased by her husband, Cameron, in 1979; four sisters, Lillian Weeks, Grace Smith, Eleanor Taylor, and Margaret Virginia Glendenning; two brothers, Walter and John Glendenning.

Visiting will be from Adams Funeral Home, King St., Miramichi, on Tuesday, only, from 2-4 &7-9 pm. Funeral service will be held from Adams funeral Home Chapel, on Wednesday, January 15, 2003, at 2:00 pm conducted by Rev. Dr. Phillip Crowell. Interment will be in the Black River Community Cemetery, later in the spring.

As expressions of sympathy, donations to the Miramichi Retired Teachers Assoc., or any charity of the donors' choice would be appreciated by the family." ...

Notes for DONALD CAMERON RUSSELL:

Cam was born and lived all his life in Point Aux Carr. He was a fisherman - smelts in the winter and salmon in the summer. At one time he fished just off-shore from where he lived but the fishing by set nets was banned and he "drifted" for salmon. He also farmed. When courting Venetia, he would often bicycle to Little Branch only to be asked to a barber to the Glendenning boys.


He played the fiddle at dances in the local school, served as an elder in St. Pals Presbyterian Church and also sang in the choir. For years he represented St. Paul's Church on the Black River Cemetery committee

101. LILLIAN ELSPETH6 GLENDENNING (JANE ELIZABETH JARDINE5 WATLING, JUBAL4, JUBAL3, JUBAL2, WATLING1 FAMILY)1 was born January 8, 1917 in Fairhaven, Conneticut, U. S. A.1, and died October 23, 2001 in Wauklehegan Manor, MacAdam, NB. She married KENNETH BRYDONE WEEKS1 December 22, 1939 in Glendenning's, Little Branch, Black River, New Brunswick1, son of ELLERY WEEKS and ALMA DEALY. He was born July 6, 1917 in Loggieville, NB1, and died September 16, 1994 in Stanley, New Brunswick1.


Notes for LILLIAN ELSPETH GLENDENNING:

Lillian was born at 3 Perkins St., Fairhaven, Conn., USA, where Dad was working at the time. She would have traveled with the family to Hampton, Virginia and Muscle Shoals, Alabama before returning to Little Branch in 1919. She attended Little Branch School for a few years but admitted that she did not like school.


In school, students required a slate cloth and water to main clean slates. Once Lillian dropped her slate cloth and instead of picking it up, tied the ankle of the student in front of her to the leg of the desk. When the teacher called the girl to come to the board, the slate cloth tripped her.


Dad was returning from Moncton; Lillian was with him and maybe others. Near Rexton, a car tried to run him off the road. Lillian thought that they were rumrunners but perhaps the rum was inside them rather than just in the car. Dad pulled in to a store/gas station and phoned the RCMP. A police car came from Newcastle. Lillian remembered riding in the police car as far as John W. MacNaughton's.


Lillian was a close friend of Maxine Macdonald and they used to visit back and forth. One of Mum's tricks was to ask Lillian to take a note over to Maxine's mother. She dutifully carried the noted a half-mile to Maxine's place. She did not know it at the time, but the note simply said "Please send Lillian home after a while."



At age 16, Lillian was glad to go to Boston to work. Ken remembers the phone call from her sister, Grace, who was living on Beacon Street at the time, saying that there was a job for her in Boston; Walter took Mum, Venetia and Lillian to catch the train at Newcastle. She arrived in Boston on September 22, 1933 and, I think, stayed with Dad's cousin, Sam Knowles, for a while. That night, she went to a dance with Grace and Roy to celebrate Grace's 22nd birthday. (Dates don't quite match)


While in Boston, Lillian did housework and looked after children first for Mrs. Fineberg, who was a singer, and later for Mrs. Piser. Her last employer was Mrs. Walker where she worked for 5-6 years. One of her addresses was "61 Mayo Avenue, Needham."


Brydone and Lillian met on April 18, 1933 while Lillian was visiting with Aunt Tine in Loggieville. There was a service in the United Church and afterward the young people played badminton. Brydone walked Lillian back to Aunt Tine's. They courted for over 6 years. Lillian came home most summers but Brydone also visited her in Boston. In 1936, Brydone and Walter traveled to Boston by train stopping to visit in Portland on the way. Brydone and his mother drove Lillian back to Boston in 1936 after Grace's wedding.


I can remember Brydone coming to court Lillian in his Dad's car (a 1935 Ford perhaps); I would hang around the front door and as they got into the car. Occasionally, Brydone would invite me to get in and go for a ride. Sometimes the ride would include a trip to the "Bayshore" and end with a cone of ice cream.


Lillian and Kenneth Brydone Weeks (July 6, 1917 - Sept 16, 1994) were married at Little Branch on December 22, 1939. by Rev. P. McK. Sampson, Minister of the Sunny Corner Presbyterian Church and they lived in Loggieville. Shortly after, Brydone enlisted in the North Shore Regiment. Lillian lived for a while in Woodstock, and Sussex as well as Camp Borden. She and the family lived in Loggieville (13 Church Lane?) while Brydone was overseas. .


Lillian was a people person. She was active in her church. She enjoyed company, was a great conversationalist and enjoyed both hearing and telling jokes. Although she did not play any musical instrument she often sang while working around the house. She also sang in the choir and I can still hear her singing The Old Rugged Cross. Lillian, Brydone and daughter, Patricia, frequently led special church services with readings and song.

Lillian was a note-maker. She maintained a record of dates of events from her community and from Black River. In 1974-75, Lillian typed 144 pages of memories of her early years. In 1995, I arranged for them to be printed and she gave copies to family and a few close friends. Among other things, Lillian remembered visiting Bird Hill to observe the "Zepplin", a German dirigible pass on its way to New York. That is the dirigible famous for having burst into flames while moored in New Jersey.



Lillian went to Boston on on September 22, 1933 and returned to NB to live in September, 1939.

The Fredericton gleaner carried the following on October 25, 2000:

Notes for KENNETH BRYDONE WEEKS:

Brydone was born in Loggieville and graduated from the Loggieville High School. For a number of years he worked for A&R Loggie Company delivering groceries and feed with one of his regular routes being Loggieville to Escuminac. People would phone in their orders and Brydone would deliver. Once or twice he took me with him and bought lunch while in Escuminac. It was quite a treat.


Brydone always wanted to farm and after leaving the army purchased a dairy farm near Harvey Station. Brydone was always active in his church and, in late 1964; Brydone accepted a call to minister in a three-point Presbyterian charge with a manse provided in Stanley; Lillian and Brydone lived there until their retirement.


After Brydone retired, Lillian usually accompanied him when he substituted throughout Southern New Brunswick for ministers who were ill or on vacation. After Brydone died, Lillian moved to Harvey Station, then with Ken and Beverley but eventually moved into a senior's home in MacAdam.


Their homes in Harvey Station and in Stanley were favourite stopping places when traveling through to Boston - as well as just for family visits. I frequently stayed there. In fact, at Easter of 1955, I was visiting when Mum phoned one night to say a letter had arrived saying that I had won a Beaverbrook Overseas Scholarship.

102. JOHN FRANKLIN6 GLENDENNING (JANE ELIZABETH JARDINE5 WATLING, JUBAL4, JUBAL3, JUBAL2, WATLING1 FAMILY)1 was born December 14, 1919 in Black River , NB ?1, and died June 21, 1986 in Black River, NB1. He married MARY EDNA BESSIE WATLING1 January 27, 1944 in Glendenning's, Little Branch, Black River, New Brunswick1, daughter of WILLARD WATLING and CLARA MACDONALD. She was born March 7, 1923 in Point Aux Carr, NB ?1.


Notes for JOHN FRANKLIN GLENDENNING:

John was born at Little Branch shortly after the family returned to Canada. He attended the Little Branch School probably for 7 or 8 years, and then he worked on the farm. Dad had not been well for a number of years so Walter and John did the farm work.


John and Walter hauled fish from Hardwicke to Chatham, cut hardwood on the "ridge" and delivered it to Loggieville and Chatham. One day they would go to Hardwicke or Baie St. Anne (including Fred Savoie's) and bring fish back to the Branch; the next day, they delivered the fish to Chatham for $5.00 per ton (two box-sled loads). They went to the woods and cut and split a cord of hardwood and the next day delivered it to Loggieville or Chatham for $5.00 including the cost of the wood.


John worked hard and put in long hours. He expected others to do their bit whether it was bringing in the wood for the stove, doing barn chores, going to the woods in winter, making hay in summer, etc. It was expected and you did it. But he was also very generous to people needing help.


In the late 1930's John, along with some other young men from Black River including Tom Harper, Theodore Fowlie, took an agricultural course in Newcastle. The course included practical farm mechanics, agriculture, book keeping, general mechanics and woodworking. As part of the course, John made a beautiful birch desk, which he finished in walnut. I also remember that he learned a new technique for killing chickens. I believe that it was then or shortly thereafter, that John rigged up a wind generator on the roof of the barn which would charge a battery and provide light.


One winter, November I believe, he and Brydone Weeks (Brydone was home on leave, probably before he went overseas) built a tractor out of an Oakland front end (a car that belonged to Alex MacDonald) and a Godferson rear end (a truck used by the A&R Loggie Co) - and they did this in Walter's old shed without the aid of welding and cutting equipment (or heat). The tractor had two transmissions. The tractor was very powerful but the too heavy for soft ground.


During the winter of 1945-46, and perhaps winters previous to this, John, Ken and Norm all worked in the woods for Hubert Matchett of Sunny Corner. They would be gone all week and I did the barn work.


As a young lad going on 17, I went to work on the boats in Chatham loading pulp and pit props. (I am sure that I got my first job based on the reputation of John, Ken and Norm.) One day we were storing 8-foot props down in the hold. They are heavy enough anyway but these were also moldy; I was struggling and John knew it. John said, "Let me take that one, Donald". And it happened many times.


For a number of years John went from farm to farm along the Miramichi pressing hay, first with a horse powered hay press but later with a press operated off a tractor. (When pressing hay at home, it was my job to keep the horse moving and to prevent it from grabbing at the hay when it went past the end of the bundle.) Bundles of hay weighed between 80 to 120 pounds. John worked all around Black River, Napan, Barnaby River, Cains River, etc. For a number of years, John owned a 3-ton Dodge truck, which I believe he bought from Hubert Matchett, and trucked pulp, pit props, gravel, lime, fertilizer, hay and Christmas trees.



In 1946-47 John and Mary built a new house on the Bob MacLean property and in the spring of 1948 opened a store in their living room. In 1968 they built a new store nearer the road and put in gas tanks. John was very entrepreneurial selling groceries, flour, feed, plumbing, furnaces and anything else to make a buck.


John received his call for military service and, I believe, went to Fredericton for a physical. He had very poor eyesight and was turned down. He probably would not have had to serve anyway, because farming was an essential service and he was needed at home. Ken and Norm were already in the Service and I was too young to take over the responsibility of farming.


When Ken and Norm were in the service, I was the only one around to help with the farm work. Since Dad died when I was very young, you might say that I learned to work under John's direction. I recall going to the woods in the winter, sitting on the bob-sled and on the load of wood on the way home, building loads of loose hay and poisoning potato bugs early in the morning to take advantage of heavy dew. John was the only one of the boys who would sing while driving to and from the fields or woods - I especially remember him singing familiar hymns.


Many of the older people in Black River will remember the 1939 Ford half-ton truck that, as I recall, John purchased from Bonnie Flanagan. It was a far cry from the old Model A. The truck had wooden cab on the back and John would take people to town on Saturday night when the stores were open. There could be a 10-12 people in the back. I think that he charged one dollar. The last thing before leaving town, he would drive up Water Street and past Ben's Canteen to see if anyone needed a lift home.


John and Mary liked to visit family and friends around the Miramichi and Bathurst. John and Mary, with Margaret and Jeannie, made their first trip to Florida over Christmas of 1958. Carol and I went with them to help with the driving. Later, they managed to visit Florida for a month most winters always stopping to see family and friend in Florida and on the way. When our children were small and we visited Black River, John always had gave the kids a candy or some change just as we were about to drive away.


I did not find an obituary, however, The Moncton Times of Monday, June 23, 1986 carried the following:

"Black River Bridge couple beaten

Man dead, wife hurt in robbery

BLACK RIVER BRIDGE (Special) An elderly Black River Bridge man is dead and his wife is in serious but stable condition following what police suspect was a robbery attempt at the couple's home late Saturday. John F. Glendenning, a local grocer, was pronounced dead on arrival at hospital, while his wife, Mary, remains in stable condition at Chatham's Hotelo Dieu Hospital.

Both are believed to have been victims of a beating by "several" persons.

Police are saying little about the attack, but confirm several persons were questioned late Sunday before being released. Robbery is presumed to be the motive for the attack at this point of the investigation.

Mary Glendenning, 62, was apparently tied up by her assailants but managed to free herself to telephone for help. No one else was in the house at the time.

The attack occurred sometime between 10 pm.and midnight Saturday. The Glendenning's store adjacent to the house wasn't broken into, according to one family member.

Newcastle RCMP are being assisted by General Investigation Section officers from Moncton, RCMP subdivision. Black River Bridge is about 10 miles from Chatham.

Born in Black River Bridge, Glendenning was the son of the late Elmer and Jane E. (Watling) Glendenning He was a grocer and farmer, a member of St. Paul's Presbyterian Church, Black River and an active member of the Black River Community Hall.

Besides his wife, the former Mary E. Watling of Point au Carr, he is survived by two daughters, Mrs. Aubrey Gibson (Margaret) of Black River Bridge, Mrs Ray Williston, (Jeannie) of Moncton; three sisters, Mrs Roy Smith (Grace) of Boston, Mass., Mrs Brydone Weeks (Lillian) of Harvey Station, Mrs Cameron Russell, (Venetia) of Point au Carr; four brothers, Walter of Moncton, Kenneth of Centre Napan, Norman of Black River Bridge, Dr. Donald of Charlottetown, P.E.I.; four grndchildren; several nieces and nephews.

The body will be at Adams Funeral Home, Chatham after 7 p.m. Tuesday. Funeral service will be at St. Paul's Presbyterian Church at a day and time to be announced. Burial will be in Black River Cemetery".


More About JOHN FRANKLIN GLENDENNING:

Notes for MARY EDNA BESSIE WATLING:

John met Mary when she attended a service at St. Paul's with her cousin, Millie MacDonald. He took Mary back to Point Aux Carr that night. John and Mary were married at the Branch on January 27, 1944 and lived at the Branch for a while. The day of, or day before, the wedding, John went in town to do some last minute shopping. It was raining and the roads were icy. An RCMP vehicle went through a stop sign and clipped John's truck.


Mary grew up and went to school in Point Aux Carr, she also attended Sunday school which was held in the school. As part of a large family, Mary took her turn doing all of the chores including those in the barn as well as the house.


Mary was a true partner with john in running their country store; John opened the store about 6:00 am while Mary got breakfast; john went in for breakfast about 8 and Mary took over in the store. It was a full-time job for both of them.

103. ELINOR JEAN6 GLENDENNING (JANE ELIZABETH JARDINE5 WATLING, JUBAL4, JUBAL3, JUBAL2, WATLING1 FAMILY)1 was born June 10, 19221, and died February 22, 1973 in Miramichi Hosp, Newcastle, NB1. She married EARL A. TAYLOR1 February 17, 19401, son of ERNEST TAYLOR and ROBERTA MCINNIS. He was born May 10, 1916 in Napan ?1, and died December 20, 1969 in Miramichi Hospital, NB1.


Notes for ELINOR JEAN GLENDENNING:

Elinor, her Dad called her Nen, was born after the family returned to Canada; she attended the Little Branch School to the end of Grade 8. She then wrote High School Entrance exams and attended high school classes in Bay du Vin.

She usually walked to Bay du Vin, a distance of 5 miles, but sometimes rode her bicycle. In the winter she boarded at Barbour Williston's. On a beautiful sunny day in early September, 1935, I walked with Elinor to my first day at school. She wrote and passed her Junior Matriculation.


By 1933, Grace, Venetia and Lillian had left home and Elinor would have been the only daughter at home to help Mum. With Dad and five boys at home, while still going to school, and without electricity or running water, the work would have been heavy, indeed.


Elinor met Earl when he worked and stayed at home. They lived at the Branch until, I think, Earl went in the army then they bought a house on Harriman Lane in Loggieville. As best I can remember, Elinor stayed in Loggieville all the time Earl was in the service.


In the summer of 1949, Elinor and Earl and family moved to Black River. Alex MacDonald had been killed in an accident and W. S. Loggie Company held the mortgage on his property. Elinor turned over their house in Loggieville to Loggie's and paid the difference - $300. I moved their belongings from Loggieville to Black River in the 1949 red Chev ½ ton truck. On one trip, we stopped to see Norm who was cutting grain in the field below John's house.


As her family got older, Elinor did housework for a number of people in Chatham and at CFB Chatham. She also worked at the Miramichi Seniors' Home in Chatham and at the Rehabilitation Centre. Elinor was active in St. Paul's Presbyterian Church being a member of both the Women's League and the Presbyterian Missionary Society, a member of the Women's Institute and an active Member of the Black River Community Hall Committee. The hall had burned and a new building was constructed. (This was the second time that the Black River Hall had burned.). She phoned me to tell me about the fire; a few days later she went into the hospital and died after a brief illness.


Once when having supper after a day digging potatoes, we were sitting at the dining room table. Uncle Jim was there because it was the custom for neighbours to exchange labour while cutting wood, threshing, picking potatoes etc. It was dark so there was an oil lamp on the table. Elinor went to reach across for butter and poked her knife through the lamp globe. Of course, the flame went out as a result of the air rushing in.


Elinor wrote regularly when I was in England and her letters were always newsy. At Christmas of 1955 - my first Christmas away from home, I recall coming back to the residence late on Christmas day to find a letter waiting for me from Elinor. (Only in England would mail be delivered on Christmas day.) She also wrote a letter every day to her son, Robert, when he lived in Toronto.

Elinor was a "high-energy" person - some may say it was a nervous energy. She was a good worker, had a quick turn of mind, was fiercely loyal, and quick to support or defend family members. She also had a temper. Once when she was young, Mum sent the youngsters upstairs to make their Valentines. (Ken says that Jamie Mortimer and Ward Godfrey were at home at the time). Elinor and Norm had a spat and Norm tried to take the scissors from her and cut his hand. Elinor enjoyed hockey and was a fan of the Montreal Canadiens. She was a good letter writer and wrote to her son, Robert, every day but two during the years he lived in Toronto. I forget the reason for the first day she did not write but the second was the day she was admitted to the hospital just before she died. The following tribute was written by Mrs. Donald Angus MacDonald.


To Elinor

Today I stood beside your silent bier,

Sweet fragrant flowers, tokens of esteem

Brought me thoughts of other days,

I see again your ready smile and kindly ways.


I feel your hand upon my arm

To aid me down the steps.

And see your keen and friendly interest

In every one you met.


You gave yourself to every worthy cause.

Your untiring energy you could not stay,

Our whole community is a richer place

Since you have passed this way.


The Master called you to a higher post

But this is not the end,

We'll meet again, and you will know

How proud I am, that I have called you friend.

C. A. MacDonald, February 24, 1973



A clipping believed to be from the Moncton Times reports:

"Mrs. E. A. Taylor

CHATHAM (Special) - The death of Mrs. Elinor Jean Taylor, widow of Earl A. Taylor of Black River Bridge, occurred at Miramichi Hospital in Newcastle Thursday after a brief illness. She was 50.

Born at Black River Bridge, she was the daughter of the late Elmer and Jane (Watling) Glendenning.

She was a member of St. Paul's Presbyterian Church in Black River, the WMS of the church, vice-president of the Miramichi Presbyterial, vice president of the Northumberland County Women's Institute.

She was employed at the Miramichi Rehabilitation Centre in Chatham.

Surviving are, two daughters, Mrs. Eugene Shea (Sandra) of Ferry Road and Mrs Benson Harris (Ginny) of Brantford, Ont.; five sons, Gerald of Beechwood, Robert of Toronto, David and Kenneth, both of Black River, and Frank of Chatham; three sisters, Mrs. Roy Smith of Malden, Mass.,Mrs. Cameron Russell of Point au Carr, Mrs. Brydone Weeks of Stanley; five brothers, Walter of Moncton, John and Norman, both of Black River, Kenneth of Centre Napan and Donald of Charlottetown; and 12 grandchildren.

The body is resting at her home with funeral arrangements to be announced."

Notes for EARL A. TAYLOR:

Earl grew up in Napan and attended the Napan school. As a young man, he worked around Napan and later did general farm work for Elmer Glendenning including haying, barn work and cutting wood in the winter. He joined the army in 194? And, as I recall, was stationed in Fredericton, New Brunswick. Earl was discharged from the army on February 15, 1946 and worked at the Chatham air base. .

Fowlie's Mill




Earl had purchased a beautiful cream, chrome and red bicycle - double bars, handle bars and all. It was a far cry from the old bicycle which Walter had as a kid and which we, and half Black River, learned to ride on. Sometimes Earl would take me on the parcel carrier; sometimes he even let me take it by myself. One evening, just at dusk, he took me across to the field just behind Uncle Jim's shed where Uncle Jim was burning piles of brush



A clipping, believed to be from the Moncton times, reports the following:


Funeral For Victim of Road Mishap

BLACK RIVER (Special) - The funeral of a Black River man who died in hospital Tuesday of injuries received when hit by a car, will be held today at 2:30 p.m. from St. Pau's Presbyterian Church here.

Earl Taylor, 53, was struck by a car in front of his home just after he steeped from a friend's vehicle, Newcastle RCMP said. The mishap occurred at 6:15 pm. and Mr. Taylor died in Miramichi Hospital, Newcastle, three hours later.

Driver of the vehicle that struck Mr. Taylor was Willard MacDougall, also of Black River. Coroner Dr. J. P. Losier has ordered an autopsy and inquest unnecessary. Black River is about 8 miles east of Chatham.

Born at Napan Bay, Mr. Taylor was a son of the late Ernest and Roberta (McInnis) Taylor. He was a tradesman's helper at CFB St. Magaret's. He was a second World War veteran and attended St. Paul's Presbyterian Church.

He is survived by his wife, the former Elinor Glendenning; two daughters, gene Shea (Sandra) of Ferry Road and Mrs. Benson Harris (Ginny) of Simcoe, Ontario; five sons, Gerald of Beechwood, Robert Toronto, David of Moncton, Frank and Kenneth at home; six sisters, Mrs. Harold Ryan of Fredericton, Mrs. Harvey Williston of Chatham, Mrs. John Williston of Bay du vin, Mrs. George MacDonald of Black River, Mrs. Edward Roach and Mrs Wilfred Turbide, both of Seven Islands; six brothers, Ronald of Redbank, Linwood of Chatham, Bud and Leo, both of Seven Islands, Alvin of Napan Bay, and Melvin of Lincoln, and seven grandchildren.

The Body is resting at his hoe where prayers will be said at 11:00 am today. The body will be taken to St. Paul's Presbyterian Church where the funeral will be held at 2:30 pm today. Interment will be in the Black River cemetery.

104. KENNETH ELMER6 GLENDENNING (JANE ELIZABETH JARDINE5 WATLING, JUBAL4, JUBAL3, JUBAL2, WATLING1 FAMILY)1 was born July 25, 1924 in Black River Br., New Brunswick1. He married JANET VERNE TAYLOR1 May 23, 1946 in Calvin Pres. Ch., Chatham, N. B., New Brunswick1, daughter of ALFRED TAYLOR and EMMA WOOD. She was born July 26, 1924 in Chatham, NB ?1.


Notes for KENNETH ELMER GLENDENNING:

Ken attended the Little Branch School to the end of Grade 8, took classes in the Bay du Vin Hall and graduated from MRHS. One year he stayed with Venetia and Cam in Point Aux Carr, went to school, and did the chores while Cam fished smelts. After High school, Ken took a business course in Newcastle


In 1943, Ken enlisted in the RCAF; he phoned Mum from Moncton and, as I recall, Walter, Mum and I saw him at the Newcastle station when the train stopped briefly. His first posting was to Toronto for basic training then to Galt for technical training as an airframe mechanic; later postings included St. John's, Quebec and Scoudouc, New Brunswick. He brought an airman from BC home for Christmas that first Christmas. After demobilization, he worked on the Miramichi including winters in the woods for Hubert Matchett and summers loading pulp and pit props on the boats.


In 1948 he took a carpentry course in Moncton and, after doing carpentry work for a while, in 1950 he re-enlisted in the RCAF and his postings included Trenton, Calgary, Armstrong, Resolute, Moncton, Scoudouc, Greenwood, Ottawa and Chatham. He retired from the air force 1984 after having a stroke.


In October 1939, Ken and I both came down with scarlet fever a week apart. I think the quarantine period was 6 weeks - a red warning sign was posted by the front door. We shared the bedroom on the ground floor. Then Ken came down with complications and I was moved to the small bedroom upstairs. That was the time that "Old Jess", our faithful old horse, was put down.


Ken did all the usual farm work including collecting sap from which Mum made maple syrup. I recall going with Ken with a horse and sled, with cream cans and the barrel from the churn, to collect sap before we went to school in the morning.


Ken has stories to tell about Venetia catching him when he tumbled out of the car on a turn in the Bathurst Road, getting pecked near an eye by a hen, and Wallace MacDonald's visit to the Little Branch School.


While in the service, Ken worked as a carpenter in the evening and on Saturdays wherever he was posted; he built his own home in Napan, Venetia's home after her fire and a home for his in-laws after their fire. He pitched in to help others building homes, barns, sheds, etc.



Ken has a great memory and likes to recount jokes and stories of yesteryear. I often check with him on items about family tree information. He attends in local political meetings. At age 80 he purchased a computer and has learned the intricacies of email, games, and the internet and later he registered on Facebook. Ken and Verne are regular participants in the seniors' card games at the Black River Hall and impromptu games either at their home or the home of friends. When in the service, he was an active curler.




More About KENNETH ELMER GLENDENNING:

Activities: Church Elder, cards, curling, cribbage, church suppers

Baptism: St.Stephens Presbyterian Church, Black River, NB

Christening: June 14, 1926, Black River, New Brunswick1

Education: Little Branch School; Miramichi Rural High,; Business course at Harkins High; carpentry at NBIT after war

Met spouse: At dance in Napan (?)

Military service: Enlisted in 1942 and trained as an airframe mechanic; re-enlisted in RCAF in 1950 as carpenter and later changed to a Clerk Engineer; he retired from AirForce from the position of specification officer.

Nationality: Canadian

Occupation: Woods work, Carpenter,RCAF

Religion: Presbyterian

Retirement: 1984, CFB Chatham as a Specifications Officer


Notes for JANET VERNE TAYLOR:

In 1946 he married Verne Taylor from Napan who he had first met at a dance. Verne taught school before she was married including school in Upper Black River.


Ken and Verne are active members of St. Paul's Presbyterian Church and Ken has been Clerk of Session for 27 years. For years they picked up Venetia and Edith Bremner every Sunday for church and still take Edith on a regular basis. Verne was also active in the Women's Missionary Society. They are active in the senior's organization and are ardent supporters of church suppers in the Miramichi. She also plays auction 45 and is a good cribbage player.





More About JANET VERNE TAYLOR:

Activities: Church, cards, cribbage,

Education: Napan School, Loggieville High School, St. Michaels Academy.

Nationality 1: Canadian

Nationality 2: Canadian

Occupation: Teacher, Nursing home administrator, Homemaker

Religion: Presbyterian

Children of KENNETH GLENDENNING and JANET TAYLOR are:

105. NORMAN PETERS6 GLENDENNING (JANE ELIZABETH JARDINE5 WATLING, JUBAL4, JUBAL3, JUBAL2, WATLING1 FAMILY)1 was born December 31, 1925 in Black River Br., N. B.1. He married DOROTHY JEAN WATLING1 September 27, 1951 in Calvin Pres. Ch., Chatham, N. B.1, daughter of WILLARD WATLING and CLARA MACDONALD. She was born October 31, 1930 in Point Aux Carr, NB1.


Notes for NORMAN PETERS GLENDENNING:

Norm was born on New Year's Eve, 1925, however, when the birth was registered; it was given as December 31, 1926. Grandmother Glendenning was a Peters hence his second name. According to Lillian, Mum and Dad had difficulty deciding between the names Norman and Robert.


Norman attended the Little Branch School, classes in the Anglican Church in Bay du Vin and the Miramichi Rural High School. Before school bus service was established, Norm used to take a horse to Bay du Vin, and later Bayside, picking up other students along the way. The horse was stabled for the day in either Goymer Williston's or Harrison Smith's barn.


At age 18, he enlisted in the army and found himself in the RCEME being sent first to Camp Utopia outside Saint John, New Brunswick and later to Woodstock, Ontario and Longueil, Quebec.


Norm was a good skater and a good dancer, and usually took me along when I first started going to dances in 1946. I also remember working with Norm at the hay; in the woods on cold and snowy Saturdays; butchering and peddling meat; shearing and dipping sheep and whatever else needed to be done.


Among Norm's stories is one about Wallace MacDonald throwing a peavey at him and Edwin Adams, trips on icy roads up river with Mr. Harper to tend to a sick horse and the first time he drove a vehicle with airbrakes.


Norman married Jean Watling from Point Aux Carr. Alton and Eric now operate the farm although Norm continues to "supervise" and run errands; most every day he makes a trip to Miramichi City. Branchview, the Glendenning farm, is the largest farm east of Miramichi and one of the largest in the Miramichi area. Branchview has been the recipient of a number of awards.


Norman and Jean always welcome family to the old family home providing a place at the table for children, siblings, grandchildren, nephews and nieces alike. He always tells visiting children where the kittens were and offers to give them one to take home. Many young people of the community earned some money and learned important work habits while working on the farm with Norman and the "boys".


Norm's early years were filled with long evenings of farm chores on top of full days driving truck for Alfred MacDonald or with John pressing hay.


For a number of years Norman served as a trustee of the Black River Community Hall including during a major renovation in the 1960's and reconstruction in the 1970''s; was a director of Agricultural Society #103 and was a Director of the Northumberland Cooperative Dairy. He participates in local political meetings. Although not one to travel, in "retirement" he takes time to visit neighbours and friends around Black River.



More About NORMAN PETERS GLENDENNING:

Activities: Church, Community Hall, Northumberland Cooperative, family gatherings

Baptism: June 14, 1926, St. Pauls Presbyterian Church, Black River ?

Education: Little Branch, Miramichi Rural Hign,

Met spouse: Abt. 1950, Dance at Mel Adams, Bayside

Military service: Bet. 1944 - 1945, # , RCEME, Utopia, Woodstock, Ont, and

Nationality: Canadian

Occupation: Aft. 1945, Dairy Farmer, truck driver.

Religion: Presbyterian


Notes for DOROTHY JEAN WATLING:

Jean was born in Point Aux Carr and attended the Point Aux Carr school. As a member of a large family, Jean did her share of house and farm work.


Norman and Jean are active in their community and are members of St. Paul's Presbyterian Church; Jean has been a member of the woman's Missionary society for more than 40 years and Norman is a Trustee.


At age 59, she got her first drivers license and often drives her sister, Mary, to Point Aux Carr for a few days in Mary's cottage - especially when other family members are home. Their home and their camp are the scene of family gatherings especially on holiday weekends



More About DOROTHY JEAN WATLING:

Activities: Church,

Education: Point Aux Carr, NB

Nationality: Canadian

Occupation: Home maker

Religion: Presbyterian

Children are listed above under (68) Dorothy Jean Watling.


106. DONALD ERNEST MALCOLM6 GLENDENNING (JANE ELIZABETH JARDINE5 WATLING, JUBAL4, JUBAL3, JUBAL2, WATLING1 FAMILY)1 was born September 8, 1929 in Little Branch, New Brunswick, New Brunswick1. He married CAROL LOUISE CHESNEY1 January 31, 1958 in St. Pauls, Black River Br., N. B., New Brunswick1, daughter of ERNEST CHESNEY and LOLA KAIFER. She was born September 7, 1936 in Henry, Illinois, U. S. A.1.


Notes for DONALD ERNEST MALCOLM GLENDENNING:

Don was born at Little Branch on September 8, 1929 and was baptized in the new St. Paul's Presbyterian Church in Black River on opening day, July 20, 1930. Like the rest of the family, he attended the Little Branch School; he also graduated from the Miramichi Rural High School.


He completed a six-month teacher training program at Teachers' College in Fredericton in June 1947 and his first teaching position was at Middle Kouchibouguac. Over the next few years he mixed teaching and study completing a PhD at Indiana University in 1964. He also taught at the Miramichi Rural High School and the New Brunswick Institute of Technology. In 1969, after a number of years with the Federal Government, he moved to Prince Edward Island to become the first President of Holland College. He also was active in a number of national professional organizations.


After retiring from Holland College in 1987, he set up his own company and engaged in educational consulting in Canada and abroad. His overseas contracts provided many travel opportunities especially to Asia and Africa.


He was bitten early by the travel bug and, either for business or pleasure, visited most every corner of Canada, most US states, and 40 countries in all. As a family, they lived in Moncton, Bloomington (Indiana), Ottawa, Vancouver and now in Charlottetown. Don and Carol continue to travel and usually spend some time each winter in the southern USA.


After retirement, Don resumed his interest in genealogy and visited Callister Hall, Scotland, where his great grandfather was born in 1807. He publishes a Family Chronicle, an informal and occasional newsletter, which, thanks to Brian, is posted on the family website. The Newsletter carries both family and personal recollections of growing up in Black River.


Retirement activities include genealogy, reading, volunteer work, travel, gardening, writing, letters to the editor - and working on his "farm". Don was awarded the Order of Canada in 1986.


Childhood memories include picking wild blueberries, mayflowers, cherries, plums, strawberries, cranberries and mushrooms; hours spent with George Watling playing in and along the brook; our water diversion projects during spring run-off; the family going to church by horse and wagon; Sunday school under the huge tree in Uncle Jim's back yard; dances in the Black River hall, at Mel Adams hall and elsewhere; Saturday nights cruising the streets in Chatham, and many more.




More About DONALD ERNEST MALCOLM GLENDENNING:

Activities: Gardening, reading, travelling, handyman, volunteering

Baptism: July 20, 1930, St. Pauls, Black River, New Brunswick1

Christening: July 20, 1930, St. Pauls, Black River, New Brunswick1

Education: Little Branch, Miramichi Rural High, Teacher's College, NBIT, Bradley Univ., Univ. of London, Indiana Univ.

Met spouse: Abt. January 1957, Blind date through Bill Comegys and Irma ?

Nationality: Canadian

Occupation: Teacher at Kouchibouguac, teacher and principal at Miramichi Rural High, Head of Teacher Training at NBIT, Federal training specialist, college president, training consultant.

Religion: Presbyterian

Retirement: 1987, Holland College, Charlottetown


Notes for CAROL LOUISE CHESNEY:

Don married Carol Chesney from Peoria, Illinois. Of the family, Carol, Brian, Janet and the grandchildren are all dual-citizens of Canada and the USA. Carol is a graduate of Peoria High School, trained as a nurse at Methodist Hospital in Peoria and nursed in Stamford Connecticut and Moncton, NB. Carol traveled from Stamford to Moncton in the middle of winter, wondering, as the train carried her through the woods of Maine and New Brunswick, where in the world she was going.


Carol likes to travel; their most exotic trip was a safari in Tanzania, Africa; they also spent two weeks as guests of the Peoples' Republic of China where Don delivered a lecture to Chinese College Directors.

Notes for MARGARET VIRGINIA GLENDENNING:

Margaret V. Gibson

1944 - 2006


Margaret Virginia Gibson, daughter of Mary and the late John Glendenning, of the Mount St. Joseph Nursing Home, and formerly of Black River Bridge, passed away peacefully and surrounded by her family, at her residence, on Sunday, May 28, 2006, at the age of 61.


Born in Chatham, she was the daughter of the late John and Mary (Watling) Glendenning. She was a homemaker and worked in the family business, Glendenning's Store, in Black River Bridge. Following her fathers death, in 1986, she assumed management of the business until its closing. She was a good and faithful member of St. Pauls Presbyterian Church, Black River Bridge; a former Sunday school teacher; a member of the Womens Missionary Society.


She is survived by two sons, Kevin Gibson (Linda), of Fredericton and Greg Gibson (Sarah), of McBride, B.C.; her mother, Mary Glendenning; one sister, Jean Williston (Ray), of Moncton; the 'joy of her life', her three grandchildren, Maeve, Austin and Jordan; three nieces, Julie MacPhail (Jason) and their daughter, Ella, of Lower Coverdale, Kathy Robichaud (Robert), of Maugerville, and Deborah Zaidi and her sons, Aadam, Aamir and Azeem, of Scarborough, Ont.; one nephew, Tim Williston (Geraldine) and their son, John, of Riverview; as well as numerous aunts, uncles, and cousins. She was predeceased by her father, John Glendenning; two infant brothers, Franklin and George.


Visiting will be at Adams Funeral Home Ltd., 140 King Street, Miramichi, on Tuesday, from 2-4 & 7-9 pm. Funeral service will be held from St. Pauls Presbyterian Church, Black River Bridge, on Wednesday, May 31, 2006, at 2 pm, conducted by Rev. Derek Krunys. Interment will be in the Black River Community Cemetery.


As expressions of sympathy, donations to the MS Society, St. Pauls Presbyterian Church or any charity of the donors' choice, would be appreciated by the family. Funeral arrangements are entrusted to Adams Funeral Home Ltd., 140 King Street, Miramichi, telephone 773-3492.

Notes for BRYAN PETER LIND:

Miramichi Leader – July 27-2009


Bryan Peter Lind Sunday, July 19, 2009, Bryan Peter Lind succumbed to his illness at St. Joseph's Hospital in Comox, B.C. Though Dad was diagnosed with terminal cancer in April 2008 & given a poor prognosis he went at it with a positive attitude and we were blessed with 16 months of quality time. While we knew someday it would - 'the end' seemed to come suddenly - thankfully for his sake it was quick, & merciful with his family by his side. Bryan was born in Camrose, Alberta May 12, 1930 - his growing years were spent between St. Albert, Alta & Kyuquot & Port Alberni on Vancouver Island. He started his working years in the Lumber Industry in Port Alberni. Dad joined the RCAF in 1950 - after 20 years of serving in Canada & Europe he returned to the forest industry. He retired as a Lumber Inspector from Tahsis in 1990 - he and Mom moved to the Comox Valley where his passion for golf was born. Bryan was predeceased by his Father & Mother - Martin & Eleanor Lind & his sister, Clarice Pownall. He leaves to mourn the love of his life, his wife Dorothy; five sons - Ian (Carol), Kevin (Carrie), Carl (Carole), Eric (Patti), Andy (Heidi); & his daughter Kathy Seaker (Greg). He will also be missed by his three closest grandchildren Shannon, Shayne, & Bryan; his 102 year old Auntie Kissa; his sister Doris Olstad (Bud); many nieces, nephews, cousins, & very dear friends. In lieu of flowers please make donations to St. Joseph's Hospital or a charity of your choice. We wish to extend our deepest gratitude to everyone involved in Bryan's medical care - with extra thanks to Dr. Bearstain, & most especially to Dad & Mom's family physician - Dr. Thomas Gornall. Memorial Service to be held on Saturday, July 25th from 11:00 am - 1:00 pm @ the Courtenay Legion, 367 Cliffe Avenue. Arrangements entrusted to Comox Valley Funeral Home, Cremation & Reception Centre, Courtenay, B.C. (250) 334-0707

191. LAWRENCE ALBERT7 SMITH (GRACE ELIZABETH6 GLENDENNING, JANE ELIZABETH JARDINE5 WATLING, JUBAL4, JUBAL3, JUBAL2, WATLING1 FAMILY)1 was born February 25, 19421, and died June 24, 1996. He married (1) DIANE NADEAU1. He married (2) SUSAN RATTIE1.


Notes for LAWRENCE ALBERT SMITH:

Judith Gaines of the Boston globe wrote the following:


"A funeral Mass will be said today for Lawrence Albert Smith, 55, a gregarious and hard=working concessioner known to his customers as "The Great one." He died on June 24 in his Somerville home of heart failure.

According to his wife, Sue (Rattie) smith, Mr. smith began selling popcorn and soda pop in the stands in Boston at age 16, after he was caught by security guards sneaking into a hockey game.

"What are you doing here." the security guard asked.

"Looking for the employment office," said Mr. Smith who was always quick with a comeback. Personnel agents subsequently hired him as a vendor.

He later warned friends "Don't ever sneak into a hockey game because they'll give you a job and you'll be there for life."

After working as a vendor in the stands, he sold souvenirs and other novelties at a concession stand for 38 years at Boston Garden. When it closed, he sold programs at the Fleet Centre. He is the only concessionaire ever named to the Boston Garden Hall of Fame.

A big, muscular man who was about six-feet tall and weighed more than 300 pounds, "The Great One" had an expansive personality. He was always ready to pitch in with whatever needed to be done, his wife said, and he liked telling jokes.

"His customers would bring him jokes, and he liked to have new ones to tell him every night." she said. "he was really good with people."

In addition to his wife, he leaves a son, Mark, of Springfield; three daughters, Julie, Melissa and Stacy, all of Somerville; his sister, Barbara Coppla, of Malden; and a granddaughter.

The funeral Mass will be said at 10:00 am at St. Ann's Church in Somerville. Burial will be in Holy Cross Cemetery in Malden."

Notes for GERALD ERNEST TAYLOR:

Gerald and Elaine (Sealy) Taylor were killed in a car accident just south of Bathurst; I have a photocopy of a clipping, believed to be from the NB Power Newsletter, which reads as follows:

"Gerald Ernest Taylor

The death of Gerald Ernest Taylor of Upper Kent, NB occurred suddenly by accident on Monday, December 19, 1994. Gerald was the son of the late Earl and Elinor Taylor of Black River Bridge, N.B.

He was employed by the New Brunswick Electric Power Commission for the past 31 years, 30 of those years in the Beechwood Generating Station. He was a member of the IBEW Local 2309. He was a member of the Royal Canadian Legion and the Perth Andover Curling Club and held various positions.

In 1964 he married Elaine Sealy of Canobie, N.B.

He is survived by four sons; Douglas, Terrence, Jeffery and Daniel, all of Upper Kent, N.B.; daughter-in-law, Lillian; two grandchildren, Joshua and infant granddaughter Asley Elaine; four brothers, Robert, Kenneth and David from Black River Bridge, N.B. and Frank of Chatham, N.B.; two sisters, Sandra (Mrs Eugene Shea) of Douglastown, N.B., and Ginny, (Mrs Benson Harris) of Brantford, Ontario, and several nephews and nieces.

The funeral service was held Dec. 22, from Kircollins Funeral Home, Bath, N.B. Rev. David Hewett officiated, and special music was presented by Winston and Isobel Morton singing Amazing Grace and Precious Lord Take My Hand.

Pallbearers were: Norman Ingraham, Jim Cummings, George Kelly, Wavel DeMerchant, Ken Dionne, and Rick St. Peter.

Interment was made in the Wharton Cemetery, Upper Kent, NB.

A reception was held at the Knights of Columbus in Bath, N.B., organized by the Upper Kent U.C.W. and Women's Institute."

LOCAL HISTORY PUBLICATIONS

By

Don Glendenning


1 Glendenning Roots


This volume provides background and photos on the life and times of Elmer and Jane (Watling) Glendenning with a focus on their years in the USA. It also lists ancestors for each as well as their descendants. (Approximately 200 pages.)


  1. Carol Louise (Chesney) Glendenning


This booklet provides background information and photos of Carol Chesney, her family and friends in Illinois. (Approximately 100 pp)


  1. Family Chronicles; Volume 1


The first Family Chronicle was published on May 5, 1997 as an informal newsletter intended to share with siblings the latest findings about relatives and ancestors. Later the chronicle became an instrument to recall life and times of the author growing up in Black River, New Brunswick, especially in the period preceding WW2. Volume 1 gathers together the first 100 newsletters; they are also available on line at www.glendenning.net/don. A second volume is in the making.


4 The Neighbourhood

At the beginning of WW2, there were about 120 households along the Black River in Northumberland County, New Brunswick. This volume identifies heads of households and their offspring; children are identified even though many had married and left home. The volume also provides ancestor charts, where known, for many of the heads of households. (Approximately 325 pages)


  1. Families (In progress)


A series of genealogy reports, mostly of ancestors of Don Glendenning, drawn from his database of 6,000 names.

1 John Glendinning

2 Jubal Watling

3 Alexander MacDonald

4 Alexander MacNaughton

5 Margaret Harley






ABOUT THE AUTHOR





Don Glendenning was born and raised in Little Branch, Black River, New Brunswick.. He attended the Little Branch School, a one-room school serving the district, and is a graduate of the Miramichi Rural High School, the first of a series of rural high schools established in New Brunswick prior to WW2.


After his studies at Teachers’ College and the New Brunswick Institute of Technology (now the New Brunswick Community College) he taught first in a one-room school in Kouchibouguac, NB and later at the Miramichi Rural High School where he served first as a teacher and later as Principal. Community involvement included Secretary to the Farm Radio Forum, a member of St. Paul’s Presbyterian Church and a Scout leader.


Most of his life was spent in education in the public schools of Mew Brunswick, at the New Brunswick Institute of Technology, as a training specialist with the Federal government and as President of Holland College in Charlottetown, PEI. Formal studies include a year at the University of London (England), Bradley University from which he received a BSc. and MSc, and Indiana University where he received his Ph.D. In 1986 he was inducted into the Order of Canada.


He is currently active in The Community Foundation of Prince Edward Island, tracing his roots, reading and puttering around in his garden and on his “farm”. For many years, he was a regular contributor to the CVA Journal, edited a newsletter for the Community Foundation of Prince Edward Island, and currently publishes the Family Chronicle, a newsletter that reports his genealogical findings and recollections of life in Black River.


Don married Carol Chesney from Peoria, Illinois and they have two children and two grandchildren.
